By serializing messages and building compact payloads, it minimizes the amount of content sent to guard models while preserving relevant metadata for accurate decisions. It also captures trace data for debugging and monitoring by emitting LLM traces and compact pipeline traces as session tool outputs. The mixin integrates with the project's provider abstraction to perform guard model calls using template-based system and user prompts sourced from the instructions helper. When a guard flags content, the mixin supports different enforcement levels: automatic blocking or asking for human approval via a provided callback. This allows operators to tune strictness per guard type (input, output, script_tool) through configuration. The parsing logic aims to be resilient by handling structured JSON responses or falling back to text heuristics to determine allow/deny outcomes. When guards allow, the mixin records usage and proceeds with the requested operation; when they block, it raises a specific GuardBlockedError to signal callers. For tool calls, the mixin enforces script_tool guards before delegating to the tools layer and forwards contextual data like session id, policies, approval callbacks, and file registry. Overall, the mixin reduces duplication, centralizes guard-related side effects (traces, session messages, usage accumulation), and enforces safety policies consistently across the agent runtime.

Key elements:
- AgentGuardMixin (class; provides guard and guarded-execution utilities).  
- _build_pipeline_trace_payload (method; compacts pipeline/tool arguments into a small trace payload).  
- _emit_llm_trace (method; produces and emits a human-readable trace of an LLM response and tool calls).  
- _truncate_guard_text (static method; caps textual payload sizes with truncation marker).  
- _guard_settings (method; reads and normalizes guard configuration from get_config()).  
- guards_enabled (method; boolean check for any enabled guard type).  
- _serialize_messages_for_guard (method; converts Message objects into condensed text for input guards).  
- _parse_guard_decision (static method; attempts JSON parsing of guard output and applies heuristics).  
- _run_guard_decision (async method; renders guard prompts and calls provider.complete to evaluate content).  
- _request_guard_approval (method; invokes an approval_callback if present to get human approval).  
- _enforce_guard (async method; enforces configured policy per guard type and emits tool outputs).  
- _complete_with_guards (async method; performs input guard, provider completion, output guard, and tracing).  
- _execute_tool_with_guard (async method; enforces script_tool guard then calls tools.execute with context).  
- External dependencies (get_config, GuardBlockedError, Message type, provider.complete, tools.execute, instructions.load/render, get_logger, json, re, asyncio).

Key logic:
1. The mixin constructs compact payloads for pipeline traces by selecting a small set of keys for known sources like planning, completion_gate, or task_contract and otherwise including only primitive-valued entries.  
2. When emitting an LLM trace it collects basic metadata (model, message count, tool call count, usage) and formats the assistant response and any tool calls into a readable output, then records it via _emit_tool_output and as a session message.  
3. Long textual payloads destined for guard evaluation are truncated by _truncate_guard_text to a configurable maximum to avoid sending excessive content to guard models.  
4. Guard configuration is read from get_config(), where each guard type returns an enabled boolean and a normalized level that is either stop_suspicious or ask_for_approval.  
5. Outbound prompt messages are serialized into numbered lines with role and truncated content by _serialize_messages_for_guard to create an input guard payload.  
6. Guard classifier outputs are parsed by _parse_guard_decision which first attempts JSON parsing, then tries to extract a JSON object from surrounding text, and finally uses keyword heuristics to infer allow/deny when JSON is not available.  
7. _run_guard_decision composes system and user messages from instruction templates, truncates the content, calls provider.complete with those messages, accumulates usage into a provided turn_usage bucket, and returns the parsed decision including raw text.  
8. Enforcement via _enforce_guard checks whether the guard type is enabled and skips empty output checks, then evaluates the guard decision and either emits allow traces, requests human approval if configured, or emits suspicious traces and returns a block result.  
9. If a guard blocks and its level is ask_for_approval, the mixin calls the approval_callback and bases the final allow/deny on that callback while emitting the outcome.  
10. _complete_with_guards first runs the input guard on the serialized messages, then performs the provider.complete call if input is allowed, accumulates usage, runs the output guard on the LLM response, emits an LLM trace, and returns the response or raises GuardBlockedError on block.  
11. _execute_tool_with_guard JSON-encodes the tool invocation details, enforces the script_tool guard for the tool call, and upon allowance delegates to tools.execute while supplying session id, policies, abort events, workspace path, approval callback, and file registry.  
12. Throughout, usage accumulation and trace emission are used to record decisions and diagnostics, and GuardBlockedError is raised to communicate enforcement failures to callers.  
13. Error handling in guard evaluation returns conservative deny verdicts when provider calls fail or when parsing fails, ensuring safety by default.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/agent_pipeline_mixin.py

Description:
1. This module defines dataclasses and an AgentPipelineMixin that provide utilities for constructing and managing DAG-backed task pipelines.  
2. It includes a TaskStatus enum and helper functions to normalize statuses, parse ISO timestamps, and coerce various task configuration values like timeouts and tool policies.  
3. The mixin can sanitize and normalize arbitrary task contract input into a bounded DAG shape with unique task identifiers, validated execution contexts, and child relationships.  
4. It computes leaf tasks and builds a stable topological execution order that respects explicit dependencies and leaf priorities.  
5. Graph refresh and rollup functions propagate child states up to parents and maintain indexed collections such as task_graph, task_order, ready/blocked/active/completed/failed sets.  
6. Activation logic moves ready leaves into in-progress, enforces a configurable parallelism limit, and stamps tasks with start and heartbeat timestamps.  
7. Runtime ticking enforces per-task timeouts, applies retry policies, converts timed-out nodes to retried or failed states, and triggers downstream activations.  
8. Progress utilities compute per-leaf and per-scope ETA estimates, format human-readable monitor output, and build planning notes for model context injection.  
9. The mixin assembles a default pipeline from user input, optional overrides or extracted JSON contracts, and can append completion checks as final dependent tasks.  
10. It exposes helpers to set progress by index, advance tasks, cancel tasks with reasons, record token usage on active contexts, and finalize pipelines on success or failure.

Purpose:
This mixin centralizes pipeline and progress management for agents that plan and execute multi-step tasks. It exists to convert loosely-structured task contracts or free-text plans into a validated, DAG-shaped execution model with bounded size and consistent identifiers. By encapsulating normalization and sanitization logic, the mixin prevents malformed user input from breaking downstream executors and enforces sensible defaults for execution context and timeouts. The topological ordering and dependency expansion allow the agent to reason about which leaf tasks are eligible for execution and to schedule them deterministically. Runtime state refresh and rollup logic provide a single source of truth for task statuses, enabling monitoring, retries, and failure handling without scattering that logic across the codebase. Activation and concurrency controls ensure the agent can run multiple branches in parallel while respecting configured limits and tracking heartbeat/start times. Timeout/tick handling implements automated retry/backoff behavior and marks terminal failures consistently so higher-level controllers can react. Progress and ETA computation make pipeline state consumable by UIs or monitoring tools and support scope-aware reporting for nested task groups. The mixin also integrates with agent telemetry by emitting formatted monitor outputs and tool events, enabling observability of planning behavior. Utility functions for token usage accounting and compact progress snapshots aid in stuck detection and budget calculation for iterative turns. Overall, the mixin isolates complex pipeline semantics so agents can focus on task logic, tool integration, and policy enforcement while relying on consistent execution semantics.

Key elements:
- TaskStatus (enum) (defines supported lifecycle states like pending, ready, in_progress, completed, failed, cancelled, timed_out).  
- TERMINAL_TASK_STATUSES (set) (quick reference for terminal state checks).  
- TaskResult (dataclass) (structured payload for task outcomes).  
- ExecutionContext (dataclass) (per-task isolated context including token usage, spawn depth, and history).  
- TaskNode (dataclass) (node schema used for DAG-capable tasks, with children and execution context).  
- _normalize_task_status (static) (normalizes various inputs into a valid TaskStatus string).  
- _parse_pipeline_timestamp (static) (parses ISO timestamps and ensures UTC tzinfo).  
- _coerce_timeout_seconds (static) (coerces timeout values and enforces defaults).  
- _coerce_tool_policy (static) (validates/normalizes tool allow/deny policy structures).  
- _sanitize_pipeline_nodes (instance) (validates and normalizes incoming task lists into a bounded DAG).  
- _iter_pipeline_nodes / _iter_pipeline_leaves / _iter_pipeline_nodes_with_parent (static) (iterators for tree traversal).  
- _topological_task_order (static) (computes a stable topological order given leaves, dependencies and priorities).  
- _refresh_pipeline_graph_state / _refresh_pipeline_task_order (static) (rebuilds graph, dependencies, order, and ready/active/blocked sets).  
- _rollup_pipeline_status (static) (aggregates child statuses up to parents).  
- _activate_pipeline_tasks (instance) (moves ready tasks into in-progress honoring parallelism).  
- _tick_pipeline_runtime (instance) (enforces timeouts, retries, and computes activations/failures).  
- _build_task_pipeline (instance) (assembly of pipeline from free text, overrides, or extracted JSON; injects checks).  
- _build_pipeline_progress_details / _format_pipeline_monitor_output (static) (ETA and formatted monitor output).  
- _emit_pipeline_update (instance) (emits planning telemetry via self._emit_tool_output and self.instructions.load).  
- Integration touchpoints (self.session, self.instructions.load, self._emit_tool_output, self.planning_enabled) (depend on host agent environment).

Key logic:
1. Input normalization begins by coercing raw values—statuses, timestamps, timeouts and tool policy payloads—into predictable Python types and shapes so downstream code can assume consistency.  
2. Sanitization traverses user-supplied task trees depth-first, assigns unique alphanumeric ids, rejects nodes without titles, bounds total nodes, and constructs default execution contexts for each node.  
3. Leaf identification walks the normalized tree to produce leaf lists; a leaf priority map is derived from traversal order to bias topological sorting stability.  
4. Dependencies are expanded by walking parent chains and resolving referenced task ids; dependencies pointing to parent groups are translated to the corresponding descendant leaf ids.  
5. A topological order is produced using a Kahn-like approach: available (no-dependency) leaves are queued and popped in priority order, and dependents are released when all dependencies are satisfied; unresolved cycles fall back to appending remaining nodes deterministically.  
6. Refreshing the pipeline graph builds task_graph and task_dependencies structures, then classifies each leaf into completed/failed/active/ready/blocked sets by examining statuses and satisfaction of dependency completions.  
7. Rollup logic recursively computes parent node status from children, marking parents failed if any child failed/timed out, completed if all children completed, or in_progress/ready/blocked based on child mixes.  
8. Activation enforces a configurable max_parallel_branches limit, selects ready leaves in task_order order, marks them in_progress, records started_at and last_heartbeat_at, updates active lists, and triggers a subsequent graph refresh and status rollup.  
9. Runtime ticking iterates active tasks to check elapsed time against per-task timeout; on timeout it either retries (incrementing retries, clearing started_at, marking pending) or marks the task failed, and collects lists of timed_out, retried, and failed ids.  
10. After state changes from ticking or completion, the pipeline is refreshed, ready dependents are activated as slots permit, and pipeline state (including possible global failure) is updated.  
11. Utilities compute progress snapshots and detect meaningful progress by comparing tool successes, unique signatures, assistant outputs, and pipeline index transitions to help with stuck detection.  
12. Progress details calculate leaf indices, completed/ready/active counts, elapsed time since pipeline creation, and ETA by averaging seconds per completed leaf when possible; scope-level progress entries are produced by walking from root to current leaf.  
13. The mixin can advance a pipeline step-by-step by completing the current active task, creating a standardized result payload, activating ready dependents, and emitting a formatted planning event through agent telemetry hooks.  
14. Finalization either marks all tasks completed and emits a completed event or cancels/marks failed tasks, applies failure reasons, and emits a failed planning update so external orchestrators can react.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/agent_file_ops_mixin.py

Description:
1. This module implements AgentFileOpsMixin which provides utilities for extracting, synthesizing, writing, verifying, and executing script and file outputs produced by an assistant.  
2. It contains helpers to parse fenced code blocks from assistant text and infer appropriate script file extensions from language tags or shebangs.  
3. The mixin can wrap user-provided Python code in a structured-result wrapper that captures stdout and writes a JSON payload describing success/data/error.  
4. It constructs deterministic per-session paths for scripts and structured JSON result files using normalized session slugs and timestamps.  
5. The module synthesizes script content by calling the configured completion pipeline with a synthesis system prompt and falls back to a deterministic scaffold on failure.  
6. It implements logic to write files via an external "write" tool, then verify the file exists by checking a set of candidate filesystem locations and retrying when necessary.  
7. The mixin can auto-run scripts using appropriate runner commands (python/node/bash/ruby) and captures structured results produced by the wrapper or raw stdout.  
8. It extracts explicit requested write targets and filename blocks from assistant output, supports multi-file extraction, and can persist those file blocks automatically.  
9. Execution events and outputs are recorded back into the session via _add_session_message and tool outputs are emitted via _emit_tool_output.  
10. The mixin also persists assistant responses into session storage and triggers optional memory sync hooks.

Purpose:
1. The primary role of this mixin is to centralize and standardize file- and script-related operations an agent might need when responding to user requests that require producing, saving, or executing files.  
2. It exists to bridge model-generated textual outputs and concrete side effects on disk and execution environments, ensuring generated scripts can be saved, executed, and their results captured reliably.  
3. By inferring file extensions and building runner commands, the mixin enables consistent auto-execution behavior across multiple scripting languages while providing a safe structured-result protocol for Python.  
4. The structured-result wrapper ensures downstream systems can programmatically inspect script outcomes without brittle stdout parsing, improving robustness of automation workflows.  
5. The verification and retry mechanism around writing files protects against transient tool failures or mismatched write paths by scanning plausible candidate locations.  
6. The auto-write and auto-run flows allow the assistant to honor explicit user requests to save or execute code without requiring additional orchestration logic elsewhere in the agent.  
7. The mixin integrates with the agent’s session, tools, and execution guard mechanisms to enforce policies, record artifacts, and annotate execution context with result metadata.  
8. It also offers utilities for list-style task synthesis, generating a worker script prompt tailored to iterate over discovered list members and saving per-item outputs deterministically.  
9. The module reduces duplication by providing common path normalization, filename scaffolding, and parsing logic for filename-prefixed blocks that other agent components can rely upon.  
10. Logging and emitted tool outputs created here make debugging and monitoring script synthesis, write, and run actions straightforward in the broader system.  
11. The mixin’s APIs return structured dictionaries and annotate session messages so higher-level task orchestration code can interpret success/failure and evolve execution context.  
12. Overall, it exists to make file creation, verification, execution, and result ingestion predictable and auditable within the agent runtime.

Key elements:
- AgentFileOpsMixin (class - container for all file/script helpers).  
- _extract_code_block(text) (extracts the first fenced code block and language).  
- _infer_script_extension(language, code) (maps language/shebang to file extension).  
- _supported_script_extension(ext) (checks supported runner extensions).  
- _build_script_runner_command(script_path) (constructs runner command for non-python scripts).  
- _build_python_runner_command(script_path, result_path) (builds command using current Python interpreter).  
- _build_structured_result_wrapper(code) (wraps Python user code to emit structured JSON results).  
- _build_structured_result_path(script_path, session_slug) (deterministic result JSON path builder).  
- _read_structured_result_payload(path) (loads structured JSON result payload).  
- _build_script_relative_path(user_input, extension, session_slug) (creates per-session script relative paths).  
- _synthesize_script_content(user_input, turn_usage) (invokes LLM to create script code with fallback).  
- _run_python_worker_for_list_task(...) (synthesizes, writes, runs, and ingests a worker script for list tasks).  
- _write_file_with_verification(...) (executes write tool, verifies file presence across candidate paths, retry logic).  
- _maybe_auto_script_requested_output(...) (auto-writes and/or runs a script when explicitly requested).  
- _maybe_auto_write_requested_output(...) (auto-saves requested file outputs and multi-file blocks).  
- _extract_requested_write_paths(user_input) (parses requested target paths from user text).  
- _extract_named_file_blocks(output_text) (parses assistant "Filename:" blocks into (filename, content)).  
- _candidate_write_paths_for_verification(requested_path, parsed_written) (enumerates paths to check).  
- External dependencies/calls: Message (llm message wrapper), self._execute_tool_with_guard, self._complete_with_guards, self._add_session_message, self._emit_tool_output, self.tools.get_saved_base_path, self.session_manager.save_session, memory.schedule_background_sync, logging via get_logger.

Key logic:
1. The file parsing helpers first attempt to detect a fenced code block and return its language token and code body, falling back to None when absent.  
2. Script extension inference maps explicit language tags to extensions and inspects leading shebang lines to decide between Python, shell, JavaScript, and Ruby defaults.  
3. For Python code, the mixin can wrap arbitrary user code into a strict structured-result wrapper that executes the user code in a contained namespace, captures stdout, and writes a JSON payload to a result file path when provided.  
4. Script and result paths are deterministically built from a normalized session slug and a timestamp to avoid clashes and to scope artifacts per session.  
5. When synthesizing missing code the mixin issues a constrained LLM prompt (via _complete_with_guards) and extracts either a fenced code block or treats the entire response as code; if synthesis fails it emits a small deterministic scaffold.  
6. Writing a file is performed by invoking an external "write" tool through _execute_tool_with_guard, recording the tool output into the session, and parsing any returned path text for later verification.  
7. Verification enumerates candidate filesystem locations (absolute path, runtime base path, saved base path, and saved-prefix adjustments) and treats the first existing file as the verified result, with a configurable number of write attempts and retry emissions when missing.  
8. Auto-run flows decide whether to run scripts by checking whether the user explicitly requested a script action and whether prior write/run tool calls already occurred in the same turn; they construct appropriate runner commands depending on extension and may attach a structured result path for Python runs.  
9. After running a script, the mixin reads the structured JSON result if present and merges structured stdout or data into the shell tool output presentation so session messages contain both human-readable and machine-structured information.  
10. Execution context is updated with artifact lists, variables for output or error, and a recorded execution event that links task ids, script paths, and success status, allowing higher-level orchestrators to consume results.  
11. The module supports extracting multiple named file blocks from assistant output by locating "Filename:" headers, normalizing and deduplicating targets, and then saving each block using the same verified write logic.  
12. Auto-write behavior also honors explicit natural-language file requests parsed from the user prompt, attempting to save the assistant response itself when no inline file blocks were supplied and reporting summaries of saved vs failed files.  
13. Throughout the flows, all tool interactions are annotated into the session via _add_session_message and _emit_tool_output, while failures and exceptions are converted into user-facing notes or returned error structures for callers.  
14. Finally, assistant responses can be persisted into session storage and trigger optional background memory syncs, ensuring that created outputs and messages are durable within the broader agent runtime.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/session_export.py

Description:
1. This module provides utilities to export and render session history for Captain Claw.  
2. It normalizes session identifiers into filesystem-safe slugs.  
3. It can truncate long text fields to a maximum character length with an explicit truncation marker.  
4. It renders chat exports as Markdown including role, timestamp, and message content.  
5. It renders monitor/tool exports as Markdown including tool name, arguments, and content.  
6. It collects pipeline trace entries from tool messages and can fall back to other tool sources when explicit traces are absent.  
7. It serializes pipeline trace entries to newline-delimited JSON (JSONL).  
8. It produces a pipeline summary Markdown that aggregates counts by source, event, and step and produces a timeline.  
9. It writes selected export files to a structured directory path under a provided base path and timestamps filenames.  
10. Export functions create an isolated snapshot of messages before writing files to disk.  

Purpose:
1. The file exists to centralize logic for transforming internal session message records into human-readable and machine-friendly export artifacts.  
2. By providing dedicated renderers for chat, monitor, pipeline, and pipeline-summary outputs it keeps export formatting consistent across the application.  
3. Normalization and truncation helpers ensure exported filenames and content are safe and bounded for file systems and downstream consumers.  
4. Collecting pipeline trace entries allows other systems or analysts to ingest a concise sequence of pipeline events.  
5. The JSONL export is intended for programmatic consumption while the Markdown exports are optimized for human review and debugging.  
6. The module encapsulates fallback logic for pipeline traces so exports remain useful even when explicit pipeline_trace messages are not present.  
7. Timestamps and session metadata are embedded in every export header to provide context and ordering information.  
8. The export_session_history function coordinates which artifacts to produce and where to place them on disk, centralizing file IO behavior.  
9. Using a snapshot copy of messages before writing prevents accidental mutation of the source message list during export.  
10. The file's role is primarily utility-focused and stateless, making it safe to call from CLI commands, web handlers, or scheduled jobs.  
11. It depends only on simple data structures and datetime utilities to remain portable and testable.  
12. Overall it exists to make session data shareable, auditable, and analyzable outside the running application.  

Key elements:
- normalize_session_id (sanitizes a raw session id into a filesystem-safe slug).  
- truncate_history_text (limits message text to a maximum length and appends a truncation marker).  
- render_chat_export_markdown (renders chat messages to a Markdown document with headers and per-message sections).  
- render_monitor_export_markdown (renders 'tool' role messages to Markdown, including tool name and serialized arguments).  
- collect_pipeline_trace_entries (extracts pipeline_trace entries or falls back to specific tool sources and annotates payloads).  
- render_pipeline_export_jsonl (serializes collected pipeline entries into newline-delimited JSON).  
- render_pipeline_summary_markdown (aggregates counts, builds summary sections, and formats a timeline of pipeline entries).  
- export_session_history (coordinates export mode selection, directory creation, snapshotting messages, and writing files).  
- now_utc and to_utc_iso (timestamp helpers imported from captain_claw.cron used in export headers).  
- json (used for serializing tool arguments and pipeline entries).  
- pathlib.Path usage (creates directories and writes files with Path.mkdir and Path.write_text).  

Key logic:
1. normalize_session_id iterates characters of the input and builds a safe token that allows alphanumeric characters plus '.', '_', and '-', replacing all other characters with '-' then stripping surrounding hyphens and defaulting to "default" for empty results.  
2. truncate_history_text returns the original string when it is within the configured limit and otherwise truncates to max_chars, rstrips trailing whitespace, and appends "... [truncated]".  
3. render_chat_export_markdown filters messages to those whose role is one of user, assistant, or system, constructs a header with export timestamp, session id and name, and then writes each message as a numbered Markdown subsection containing role, timestamp, and content.  
4. When no chat messages are present the chat renderer emits a short placeholder indicating none were found.  
5. render_monitor_export_markdown filters messages to role "tool", attempts to JSON-serialize tool argument dicts (falling back to str on failure), and includes tool name, argument JSON, timestamp, and content for each entry.  
6. collect_pipeline_trace_entries first selects tool messages whose tool_name equals "pipeline_trace" (case-insensitive) and converts their tool_arguments into payload dictionaries augmented with seq, timestamp, session_id, and session_name.  
7. If no explicit pipeline_trace entries are found the collector scans tool messages for fallback sources ("planning", "task_contract", "completion_gate"), annotates the payload with a source field, and includes them in the returned entries.  
8. render_pipeline_export_jsonl simply joins JSON-serialized entries with newlines to produce a JSONL-formatted string suitable for programmatic consumption.  
9. render_pipeline_summary_markdown computes aggregates by source, step, and event, determines first and last timestamps, sorts aggregated lists by frequency and name, and emits sections for sources, planning events, completion/contract steps, and a timeline.  
10. The timeline formatting iterates entries and composes a concise single-line description per entry that may include seq, timestamp, source, event, step, progress (leaf_index/leaf_tasks), remaining count, current path, and eta text when those fields are present.  
11. export_session_history validates the requested mode against allowed keys, normalizes the session id for filesystem use, creates an export directory under saved_base_path/showcase/<safe_session>/exports, and snapshots messages into a shallow-copied list before rendering.  
12. Based on the mode it invokes the appropriate renderers to produce chat, monitor, pipeline (JSONL), and pipeline-summary Markdown files named with a timestamp stamp and writes them with Path.write_text, collecting and returning the list of written Paths.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/instructions.py

Description:
1. This module provides utilities to load and render LLM instruction templates from disk.  
2. It implements a two-layer override system where personal templates in a user directory take precedence over system defaults.  
3. A module-level default personal directory (~/.captain-claw/instructions) is defined and expanded to an absolute Path.  
4. The _SafeFormatDict helper class ensures that unknown format placeholders are left unchanged instead of raising KeyError.  
5. The InstructionLoader class encapsulates resolution of base and personal directories, caching, and file lookup logic.  
6. InstructionLoader._resolve_base_dir determines the system instructions directory from an explicit argument, an environment variable, or a package-relative default.  
7. The loader prefers a personal override file when returning the effective path for a named template.  
8. Loading a template reads the file text, strips surrounding whitespace, caches the content, and raises FileNotFoundError if the file does not exist.  
9. Rendering a template substitutes provided variables using str.format semantics while preserving unknown placeholders.  
10. The module is written to be simple, filesystem-driven, and to avoid altering system defaults when personal edits are made (e.g., via a web UI).

Purpose:
1. The file exists to centralize safe and predictable loading and rendering of instruction templates used by the application.  
2. It provides an explicit override mechanism so users can customize instructions locally without changing bundled defaults.  
3. By resolving base directories from arguments, an environment variable, or a package default it supports flexible deployment and developer workflows.  
4. The caching behavior reduces repeated disk reads for frequently used templates, improving performance.  
5. The SafeFormatDict approach prevents rendering failures when callers omit optional variables, making template usage more robust.  
6. Raising FileNotFoundError with a clear message informs callers when a requested template is missing and suggests where to place files.  
7. The loader preserves the original system templates intact by design, ensuring that automated updates or packaged defaults are not overwritten.  
8. Its responsibilities are limited to filesystem resolution, text loading, simple placeholder substitution, and override detection.  
9. The class-level API (is_overridden, load, render) offers a small surface for other parts of the project to reliably obtain instruction text.  
10. Support for CAPTAIN_CLAW_INSTRUCTIONS_DIR via environment variable allows CI, tests, or deployments to point to alternative instruction sets.  
11. The module intentionally avoids a complex templating engine to keep rendering behavior predictable and easy to inspect.  
12. Overall, it exists to make instruction templates discoverable, editable by users, and safe to render into LLM prompts.

Key elements:
- _PERSONAL_DIR (Path default for per-user instruction overrides).  
- _SafeFormatDict (dict subclass with __missing__ to return "{key}" for unknown placeholders).  
- InstructionLoader (main class encapsulating template discovery and rendering).  
- InstructionLoader.__init__ (initializes base_dir, personal_dir, and in-memory cache).  
- InstructionLoader._resolve_base_dir (static method to choose base instructions directory via arg, env var, or package default).  
- InstructionLoader._path (returns effective Path for a template, preferring personal override).  
- InstructionLoader.is_overridden (checks existence of a personal override file).  
- InstructionLoader.load (reads, strips, caches template content, raises FileNotFoundError if absent).  
- InstructionLoader.render (performs str.format_map substitution using _SafeFormatDict).  
- CAPTAIN_CLAW_INSTRUCTIONS_DIR (environment variable consulted when resolving base directory).  
- Path.read_text (used to read template file contents).

Key logic:
1. On instantiation the loader determines its base directory by checking an explicit argument first, then the CAPTAIN_CLAW_INSTRUCTIONS_DIR environment variable, and finally falling back to a package-relative instructions directory.  
2. The personal directory is resolved to an absolute path either from an explicit argument or the module-level default (~/.captain-claw/instructions).  
3. The loader maintains a simple in-memory cache keyed by template name to avoid repeated filesystem reads.  
4. When asked for a template path, the loader constructs the candidate personal file and returns it if it exists; otherwise it returns the corresponding file from the resolved base directory.  
5. is_overridden is a thin convenience that tests for the presence of a personal override file.  
6. load checks the cache and returns cached content when available, otherwise it determines the effective path and verifies the file exists.  
7. If the file does not exist load raises FileNotFoundError with a message that indicates where to add the missing template.  
8. When reading a template file, load uses Path.read_text with UTF-8 and strips surrounding whitespace before caching the result.  
9. render obtains the template via load, converts provided substitution variables to strings, and calls str.format_map with a _SafeFormatDict to perform placeholder substitution.  
10. _SafeFormatDict.__missing__ ensures that any placeholder without a provided value remains in the output wrapped by braces, preventing exceptions.  
11. This design makes rendering tolerant to missing variables while still allowing explicit substitutions to be expanded.  
12. The overall control flow is straightforward: resolve directories at init, prefer personal files at lookup, cache loaded contents, and render with safe placeholder handling.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/onboarding.py

Description:
1. This module implements an interactive onboarding workflow for first-time Captain Claw setup.  
2. It provides utilities to decide whether onboarding should run and to persist a completion marker.  
3. The module uses a JSON state file to record completion and reads/writes it under a ~/.captain-claw directory by default.  
4. It inspects local and global configuration files to avoid running onboarding when configuration already exists.  
5. A terminal-based wizard built with rich prompts the user to select config destination, model provider, model id, API keys, provider base URL, workspace path, safety guard settings, web search API key, and Telegram integration.  
6. Provider selection includes normalization and aliasing for common names and a default provider order and suggested models are presented.  
7. The wizard loads and saves a Config object from/to YAML and updates fields such as model provider, model id, API key, base URL, workspace, tools, telegram and guard settings.  
8. It supports storing sensitive tokens conditionally in the config file or leaving them to environment variables.  
9. Non-interactive terminals are handled by skipping or raising an error when interactive mode is required.  
10. At the end the wizard persists the configuration and marks onboarding as completed, printing a summary panel to the console.  

Purpose:
1. The file exists to guide users through initial setup of Captain Claw in a discoverable and consistent manner.  
2. It centralizes logic for deciding when onboarding should run so first-time and automated installs behave correctly.  
3. By detecting existing local or global configuration and a persisted onboarding marker it avoids redundant prompts.  
4. The interactive wizard ensures key runtime settings are captured in a user-editable YAML config file or left to environment variables as desired.  
5. Using the rich library the module presents tables, prompts, confirmations and summary panels for a user-friendly terminal experience.  
6. Provider normalization and defaults make it easier for users to choose a model provider without memorizing exact provider identifiers.  
7. The module encapsulates decisions about where to save configuration (global vs workspace) to fit different usage patterns.  
8. It also handles optional integrations like Brave Search and Telegram, letting users decide whether to store tokens in config or keep them in environment variables.  
9. Persisting onboarding completion avoids repeating the wizard and allows non-interactive environments to skip onboarding automatically.  
10. The code updates Config fields and writes YAML using Config.save to ensure settings are applied consistently with the rest of the application.  
11. The module is designed to be safe with secrets by prompting for password input and providing opt-out choices for storing credentials.  
12. Overall it provides a reproducible, interactive entrypoint for initial configuration and reduces friction for new users to get the application running.  

Key elements:
- _ONBOARDING_STATE_FILENAME (string constant; name of JSON file used to record onboarding completion)  
- _PROVIDER_ORDER (tuple; ordered list of supported providers)  
- _PROVIDER_LABELS (dict; human-readable labels for providers)  
- _PROVIDER_DEFAULT_MODELS (dict; suggested default model ids per provider)  
- _PROVIDER_ALIASES (dict; simple aliases mapping common names to canonical provider keys)  
- _resolve_state_path(state_path) (function; resolves and expands the path to the onboarding state file)  
- _resolve_global_config_path(global_config_path) (function; resolves global/default config path)  
- is_onboarding_completed(state_path) (function; checks and parses state JSON to determine completion)  
- mark_onboarding_completed(state_path) (function; writes completion payload with timestamp to state file)  
- has_existing_configuration(cwd, global_config_path) (function; checks for local or global config files)  
- should_run_onboarding(...) (function; central decision function combining flags and existence checks)  
- can_run_onboarding_interactively() (function; checks tty interactivity)  
- _normalize_provider(provider) (function; normalizes provider names and applies aliases)  
- _select_config_path(console, config_path) (function; interactive choice between global and local config paths)  
- _select_provider(console, current_provider) (function; interactive provider selection with suggested models)  
- run_onboarding_wizard(config_path, state_path, require_interactive) (function; main interactive wizard integrating all prompts and saving)  
- External dependencies: rich (Console, Panel, Prompt, Confirm, Table), captain_claw.config.Config, DEFAULT_CONFIG_PATH, LOCAL_CONFIG_FILENAME, json, datetime.UTC.  

Key logic:
1. Path resolution helpers first expand provided path arguments or fall back to default locations: the onboarding state file in ~/.captain-claw and the global config via DEFAULT_CONFIG_PATH.  
2. is_onboarding_completed loads the state JSON if present and returns True only when the payload contains a truthy "completed" key, guarding against read/parse errors.  
3. mark_onboarding_completed creates the parent directory as needed and writes a JSON payload containing a completed flag and an ISO8601 UTC timestamp.  
4. has_existing_configuration checks both the current working directory for a local config filename and the resolved global config path to determine pre-existing setup.  
5. should_run_onboarding combines explicit force, the onboarding state marker, an explicitly provided config_path existence, and presence of existing configuration to decide whether to run.  
6. can_run_onboarding_interactively uses sys.stdin.isatty and sys.stdout.isatty to ensure the wizard only runs in terminals, and callers may choose to raise if interactivity is required.  
7. Provider normalization maps aliases and lowercases input, defaulting to "openai" if an unrecognized provider is given; provider ordering and default models are used to set sensible defaults in prompts.  
8. _select_config_path constructs a two-row Table presenting global and local config destinations, computes a default option based on which paths already exist, and prompts the user to choose where to save configuration.  
9. _select_provider similarly shows provider options with labels and suggested model names, computes the default selection based on the current configured provider, and returns the selected canonical provider key.  
10. run_onboarding_wizard orchestrates the interactive flow: it checks interactivity, prints an intro Panel, selects the target config path, loads Config.from_yaml, then prompts for provider, model name, API key storage, base URL, workspace path, safety guards, Brave Search key, and Telegram configuration.  
11. The wizard conditionally prompts for sensitive values (API keys, Telegram token) using password-style Prompt when the user opts to store them, otherwise it leaves those fields blank to encourage environment variable usage.  
12. User responses are summarized in a Table; the wizard requires confirmation before applying changes and will abort without saving if the user declines.  
13. When saving, the code updates the Config object fields (model, workspace, tools, telegram, guards) according to collected answers, toggling guard enablement and levels based on the user's choice, then calls cfg.save to persist YAML.  
14. After persisting configuration, the wizard marks onboarding completed via mark_onboarding_completed, prints a completion Panel, and returns the path to the saved configuration or None if the user skipped saving.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/cron.py

Description:
1. This module provides utilities for parsing simple cron-like schedule expressions and for computing upcoming run times.  
2. It defines a mapping for weekday name variants to numeric weekday indices.  
3. It supplies helpers to obtain the current UTC time and to serialize/parse ISO datetimes with timezone normalization.  
4. A private helper parses HH:MM strings and validates their format using a regular expression.  
5. The parse_schedule_tokens function accepts tokenized schedule arguments and returns a normalized schedule dict plus how many tokens were consumed.  
6. Supported schedule forms are interval-based ("every <Nm|Nh>"), daily ("daily HH:MM"), and weekly ("weekly <day> HH:MM").  
7. The schedule_to_text function converts a schedule dict back into a compact human-readable string.  
8. The compute_next_run function computes the next UTC datetime for a given schedule, handling intervals, daily, and weekly schedules.  
9. The module consistently works with timezone-aware UTC datetimes and adjusts naive ISO inputs to UTC.  
10. It raises ValueError on invalid input formats or unsupported schedule types.

Purpose:
1. The file exists to centralize simple scheduling logic used by other parts of the project that need to run tasks on a regular cadence.  
2. It provides a small, self-contained parser so callers can accept human-friendly schedule specifications and turn them into machine-readable schedule dictionaries.  
3. By offering conversion to and from text, it supports both user-facing display and internal computation without duplicating formatting logic.  
4. The timezone-aware helpers ensure that schedules and computed run times are consistently treated in UTC, reducing ambiguity across environments.  
5. Validation and clear error messages prevent malformed schedules from propagating further into scheduling logic.  
6. The weekly weekday normalization accepts many common textual forms (e.g., "mon", "monday", "tues") to improve usability.  
7. Interval schedules allow simple repeating tasks specified in minutes or hours without needing a full cron expression parser.  
8. Daily and weekly schedules cover common scheduling needs for tasks that should run at specific times of day or on specific weekdays.  
9. The compute_next_run implementation handles edge cases like scheduling a time that has already passed today by advancing to the next valid occurrence.  
10. Keeping parsing, formatting, and next-run computation together makes it straightforward for callers to validate user input, show it back to users, and schedule execution.  
11. The module intentionally remains small and dependency-free (beyond the standard library) so it can be embedded or reused in different runtime contexts.  
12. Errors are expressed with exceptions to let calling code decide how to report or recover from invalid schedules.

Key elements:
- WEEKDAY_MAP (dict mapping many weekday name variants to weekday integers).  
- now_utc() (returns timezone-aware current UTC datetime).  
- to_utc_iso(value: datetime) (serializes a datetime to an ISO string in UTC).  
- parse_iso(value: str) (parses ISO datetime strings and normalizes to UTC, makes naive datetimes UTC-aware).  
- _parse_hhmm(text: str) (private helper that validates and parses "HH:MM" strings via regex).  
- parse_schedule_tokens(tokens: list[str]) -> (dict, int) (parses tokenized schedule input and returns schedule dict and tokens consumed).  
- schedule_to_text(schedule: dict) -> str (renders schedule dict to compact human-readable text like "every 15m" or "daily 09:00").  
- compute_next_run(schedule: dict, now: datetime | None) -> datetime (calculates the next run time in UTC for the given schedule).  
- re.fullmatch (regular expression use in time parsing and interval token validation).  
- datetime, timedelta, UTC (standard library datetime utilities used throughout).

Key logic:
1. The module normalizes time handling around UTC: now_utc returns a timezone-aware current instant and parse_iso guarantees parsed values are UTC-aware.  
2. _parse_hhmm uses a regular expression to strictly enforce hours between 0–23 and minutes between 00–59, raising ValueError on mismatch.  
3. parse_schedule_tokens inspects the first token to determine schedule type, supporting "every", "daily", and "weekly" branches; it raises informative ValueError messages for incorrect usage.  
4. For "every" schedules it expects a numeric interval followed by a unit character ('m' or 'h'), converts the numeric part to an integer, enforces interval > 0, and returns a dict with type "interval" and unit normalized to "minutes" or "hours".  
5. For "daily" schedules it parses a single HH:MM argument and returns a dict containing hour and minute.  
6. For "weekly" schedules it validates the weekday token against WEEKDAY_MAP, parses the HH:MM time, and stores both a numeric weekday and a three-letter day string.  
7. schedule_to_text inspects the schedule type and formats it back to a compact textual representation, choosing "m" or "h" suffixes for intervals and zero-padded HH:MM for daily/weekly entries.  
8. compute_next_run handles each schedule type distinctly: interval simply adds a timedelta based on minutes or hours to the current time.  
9. For daily schedules compute_next_run constructs a candidate datetime by replacing the current date's hour/minute and, if the candidate is earlier than or equal to the current time, advances it by one day.  
10. For weekly schedules it computes days_ahead with modulo arithmetic ((target_day - current.weekday()) % 7), builds a candidate by adding that many days and replacing hour/minute, and if the candidate is not strictly after current it advances by 7 days.  
11. All computed candidates are timezone-aware UTC datetimes; microseconds and seconds are zeroed when building candidate times to provide precise scheduling semantics.  
12. Unknown schedule types in parse_schedule_tokens or compute_next_run result in ValueError to ensure callers handle unsupported formats explicitly.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/runtime_context.py

Description:
1. This module defines shared mutable runtime state used by the interactive session components.  
2. It declares a PlatformState dataclass that groups per-platform bridge, user, and task metadata.  
3. It declares a RuntimeContext dataclass that aggregates the global agent, UI, command and followup queues, cron tracking, and per-platform PlatformState instances.  
4. RuntimeContext provides default factory values for command and followup queue managers and for three platform state instances (telegram, slack, discord).  
5. Each PlatformState contains fields for bridge objects, polling tasks, approval/pairing dictionaries, configuration, and enablement state.  
6. The file imports Agent and TerminalUI type hints and relies on CommandQueueManager and FollowupQueueManager for queue defaults.  
7. It initializes a telegram_command_specs list containing tuples of command names and help text used for Telegram integration.  
8. RuntimeContext exposes a get_platform_state method that returns the PlatformState for a given platform name and raises on unknown names.  
9. It also exposes a platform_names method that returns the list of supported platform names.  
10. The module is focused on in-memory structures and contains no I/O operations or network interactions itself.

Purpose:
1. The file exists to centralize and share mutable runtime state across modules that implement the interactive session and multi-platform bridges.  
2. By packaging state into dataclasses, it makes it easier to pass a single context object through different modules without relying on numerous global variables.  
3. The PlatformState dataclass isolates per-platform concerns like approved users, pending pairings, polling tasks, and platform-specific offsets and configuration.  
4. The RuntimeContext includes typed references to the core agent and terminal UI so modules can interact with those primary components consistently.  
5. Providing default factories for CommandQueueManager and FollowupQueueManager ensures queue instances are available wherever a context is instantiated.  
6. The predefined telegram_command_specs supplies a canonical set of Telegram command names and descriptions that multiple modules can reference for registration or help text generation.  
7. Centralizing cron-related fields (running job ids, poll interval, and last execution tracking) gives a common place to coordinate scheduled work across the application.  
8. The explicit mapping method get_platform_state enforces valid platform names and provides a single lookup point for platform-specific state.  
9. Returning a fixed platform_names list documents the supported platforms and can be used by UI or registration flows to enumerate available backends.  
10. The dataclass-based approach also simplifies serialization or snapshotting of runtime state if other modules implement persistence.  
11. The module separates structural state concerns from behavioral implementations such as bridges or queue processing, keeping responsibilities clear.  
12. Overall, the file provides a lightweight, type-annotated contract for how runtime components should access and mutate shared session state.

Key elements:
- PlatformState (dataclass grouping per-platform fields like bridge, poll_task, approved_users, pending_pairings, offsets, config).  
- RuntimeContext (dataclass aggregating agent, ui, command_queue, followup_queue, cron fields, and per-platform states).  
- telegram_command_specs (list of (command, description) tuples used for Telegram commands).  
- get_platform_state(platform: str) (method returning the matching PlatformState or raising ValueError).  
- platform_names() (method returning ["telegram", "slack", "discord"]).  
- CommandQueueManager (imported dependency used as default factory for command_queue).  
- FollowupQueueManager (imported dependency used as default factory for followup_queue).  
- Agent, TerminalUI (imported types referenced in the RuntimeContext signature).  
- asyncio.Task typing usage (poll_task typed as asyncio.Task or None).

Key logic:
1. The module's core logic is organizing runtime values into two dataclasses so that other modules receive a single context object containing all shared mutable state.  
2. PlatformState defaults create containers for bridge references, task handles, approval and pairing dictionaries, and platform keys without performing initialization beyond empty values.  
3. RuntimeContext constructs command and followup queue manager instances via default_factory, ensuring those queues exist whenever a context is created.  
4. The per-platform fields (telegram, slack, discord) are each initialized by a lambda default_factory that sets platform-specific name and persistent state keys.  
5. The telegram_command_specs list is created at module initialization and serves as a static reference for command names and help text rather than being computed at runtime.  
6. get_platform_state implements a simple mapping from the platform string to the corresponding PlatformState attribute and raises ValueError for unknown names, enforcing valid usage.  
7. platform_names returns a hard-coded list of supported platforms, providing a stable enumeration used by other code paths.  
8. Cron-related fields in RuntimeContext (cron_running_job_ids, cron_poll_seconds, last_exec_seconds, last_completed_at) are stored for coordination but are not manipulated in this file.  
9. The module uses type annotations to document expected object types (Agent, TerminalUI, asyncio.Task) without performing type enforcement at runtime.  
10. Default values for fields such as approved_users and pending_pairings use field(default_factory=...) to ensure separate mutable containers per context instance.  
11. No asynchronous logic or background task control is implemented here; poll_task fields are placeholders for tasks started and managed elsewhere.  
12. The design favors explicit, minimal behavior in this file: it provides state containers and simple accessors while delegating operational logic to other modules.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/orchestrator_cli.py

Description:
1. This module implements a headless orchestrator runner intended for CLI, cron, and programmatic invocation.  
2. It defines utility functions to print status updates and broadcast events to stderr for monitoring.  
3. The core async function run_orchestrator_headless prepares configuration, initializes an Agent, and constructs a SessionOrchestrator.  
4. The function supports running either a named saved workflow or an ad-hoc prompt, with optional model, provider, and parallelism overrides.  
5. It ensures workspace and session directories exist before execution and applies configuration overrides when provided.  
6. The module collects execution results into a structured dictionary containing ok, result, workflow_name, output_path, and error fields.  
7. It handles workflow loading, execution, and orchestration synthesis, and ensures orchestrator shutdown in a finally block.  
8. A synchronous CLI entrypoint cli_orchestrate is provided to parse arguments, configure logging, and invoke the async runner.  
9. An auxiliary async function _list_workflows_cli lists saved workflows and prints a simple table for the --list flag.  
10. When executed as a script, the module runs the CLI entrypoint to support command-line usage of the headless orchestrator.

Purpose:
1. The file exists to provide a lightweight, non-interactive way to run Captain Claw orchestrations outside of the web server or TUI.  
2. It enables scheduled or automated execution patterns such as cron jobs and CI tasks by exposing a stable CLI and a programmatic async API.  
3. The module centralizes startup concerns for headless runs, including loading and applying configuration, ensuring workspace directories, and configuring logging.  
4. It ties together higher-level components by creating and initializing an Agent for LLM interactions and a SessionOrchestrator for task orchestration.  
5. The design allows overriding model and provider settings, and adjusting parallel execution parameters without changing persistent configuration files.  
6. The module provides status and broadcast callbacks so that runtime events and task progress can be observed in stderr or suppressed for quiet operation.  
7. It returns structured results suitable for consumption by scripts or other programs, including JSON output when requested.  
8. Error handling and finalization logic ensure orchestrator.shutdown is always attempted, reducing resource leaks and leaving persistent state consistent.  
9. The CLI surface includes utility modes such as listing saved workflows, making it practical for operators to inspect available automations.  
10. By exposing both an asyncio-friendly function and a simple CLI wrapper, the file supports integration into other Python codebases or shell-based automation.  
11. The module limits its responsibilities to orchestration setup and invocation, delegating decomposition, execution, and persistence to Agent and SessionOrchestrator components.  
12. It exists to make headless orchestration accessible and scriptable while preserving the same orchestration semantics used in interactive modes.

Key elements:
- _print_status (function) (writes formatted status lines to stderr).  
- _print_event (function) (formats and writes broadcast event details to stderr).  
- run_orchestrator_headless (async function) (main headless orchestration runner; accepts workflow_name, prompt, config and overrides).  
- cli_orchestrate (function) (argparse-based CLI entrypoint that parses flags and invokes the async runner).  
- _list_workflows_cli (async function) (lists saved workflows and prints a simple table).  
- Config (captain_claw.config.Config) (used via Config.from_yaml and Config.load to obtain configuration).  
- set_config (function) (applies the loaded configuration globally).  
- Agent (captain_claw.agent.Agent) (instantiated and initialized to provide LLM provider and synthesis/decomposition).  
- SessionOrchestrator (captain_claw.session_orchestrator.SessionOrchestrator) (created to load and execute workflows or orchestrate prompts).  
- configure_logging and get_logger (captain_claw.logging) (used to configure and obtain a module logger).  
- asyncio.run, json.dumps, argparse, and sys.stderr (standard library utilities used for execution, output formatting, CLI parsing, and error reporting).  
- Orchestrator interactions: load_workflow, execute, orchestrate, list_workflows, and shutdown (methods invoked on SessionOrchestrator).

Key logic:
1. The module first validates inputs for the headless runner, ensuring either a workflow name or an ad-hoc prompt is provided.  
2. It loads configuration either from a provided YAML path or the default Config.load, and applies model/provider overrides if specified.  
3. The resolved workspace path and the session file parent directories are created to guarantee necessary filesystem state prior to execution.  
4. Status and broadcast callbacks are chosen based on the quiet flag; when not quiet, they point to functions that emit structured stderr messages.  
5. An Agent instance is constructed with the status callback and awaited for initialization so that provider information and LLM capabilities are ready.  
6. A SessionOrchestrator is created using configuration values (max_parallel, max_agents) and the agent's provider, and is configured with the same callbacks for status and broadcasts.  
7. The runner prepares a result_data dictionary to capture outcome metadata and uses try/except/finally to manage execution and cleanup.  
8. If a workflow_name is given, the orchestrator.load_workflow method is invoked and its success verified; failure short-circuits and returns an error result.  
9. For a loaded workflow, orchestrator.execute is awaited to perform the task execution and produce a synthesis string stored in result_data.  
10. For an ad-hoc prompt, orchestrator.orchestrate is called to both decompose and execute the prompt and produce synthesis output, with the orchestrator keeping track of an internal workflow name.  
11. Any exceptions raised during orchestration are logged via the module logger and their messages are placed into the result_data error field.  
12. The finally block always calls orchestrator.shutdown to ensure agents and worker tasks are cleaned up before returning.  
13. The synchronous CLI wrapper builds an argparse parser with a mutually exclusive group for workflow vs prompt and accepts flags for config, model, provider, parallelism, quiet, JSON output, and listing workflows.  
14. When invoked with --list, the CLI runs _list_workflows_cli which loads configuration, ensures workspace directories exist, constructs a SessionOrchestrator, and prints a table of saved workflows returned by list_workflows.  
15. Otherwise the CLI runs the async runner via asyncio.run, then either prints the raw synthesis result, outputs JSON when requested, or writes an error to stderr and exits with a non-zero status on failure.

---

7. It defines an __all__ list that explicitly exports Config, main, and __version__.  
8. The module does not define additional functions or classes.  
9. Its top-level code executes those imports when the package is imported.  
10. Overall, it centralizes a small set of public names and package metadata.

Purpose:

3. Exposing __version__ at package level makes it easy for other code to check the package version.  
4. The module docstring and __author__ provide lightweight documentation about the package identity.  
5. Defining __all__ controls what is imported when users perform from captain_claw import *.  
6. Centralizing these exports reduces the need for consumers to import deeply from submodules.  
7. The file keeps the package surface minimal and intentional by only exposing specific symbols.  
8. It also ensures that importing the package triggers import of the referenced submodules, which may perform their own initialization.  
9. This behavior makes the root package a convenient entry point for code that relies on Config or main.  
10. The initializer is typically small and stable, so it changes infrequently compared to submodules.  
11. Placing metadata like __version__ here supports packaging tools and runtime introspection.  
12. Overall, the file exists to simplify access to core components and to provide basic package-level metadata.

Key elements:
- Module docstring (package description string).  
- __version__ (string constant "0.1.0").  
- __author__ (string constant with the author's name).  
- Config (re-exported symbol imported from captain_claw.config).  
- main (re-exported symbol imported from captain_claw.main).  
- __all__ (list controlling exported names for from ... import *).  
- Top-level import dependencies (captain_claw.config, captain_claw.main).

Key logic:

4. The from captain_claw.main import main statement similarly imports main and binds it.  
5. These import statements execute at import time, causing the referenced submodules to be imported and initialized.  
6. The __all__ list is defined to explicitly list the public names that should be exported on from captain_claw import *.  
7. No functions or classes are defined here, so the module's runtime behavior is limited to these assignments and imports.  
8. Consumers importing Config or main from the package root will trigger these bindings without importing the submodules directly.  
9. Because the module re-exports names, it acts as an indirection layer between callers and the actual implementation modules.  
10. The file performs no error handling or conditional logic around the imports; import failures will propagate to the importer.  
11. Because it performs imports at top level, it should remain minimal to avoid heavy side effects during package import.  
12. Overall, the control flow is straightforward: evaluate docstring, assign metadata, import and bind exported names, and set the __all__ sequence.

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/agent_model_mixin.py

Description:
1. This mixin provides runtime model resolution and session model selection helpers for an Agent class.  
2. It normalizes provider keys and maps common aliases to canonical provider names.  
3. It refreshes and stores runtime model details such as provider, model, temperature, max_tokens, base_url, source, and id.  
4. It exposes a method to retrieve a copy of the current runtime model details for UI or status display.  
5. It builds an allowlist of models from configuration with a fallback to the configured default model.  
6. It resolves a user-supplied selector to an allowed model by id, numeric index, or provider:model patterns.  
7. It applies a selected model option by instantiating a provider via create_provider and switching the global provider with set_provider.  
8. It reads and writes session metadata to persist session-specific model selections and includes timestamps for updates.  
9. It prevents leaking a default-configured base_url into a differently selected provider when applying a session option.  
10. It exposes an asynchronous method to set the session model, handle persistence, and return success/error messages.  

Purpose:
1. The file centralizes logic for choosing and activating LLM models at runtime for agents that can use different providers and model configurations.  
2. It exists to bridge static configuration (cfg.model) and dynamic session-level overrides, enabling per-session model selection.  
3. By normalizing provider keys and mapping aliases, it makes user input and config entries tolerant to common provider names.  
4. The mixin encapsulates the rules for constructing the allowed model list and for falling back to a default configuration when no allowlist is present.  
5. It provides deterministic resolution of selectors by id, index, or provider/model patterns so user commands can select models reliably.  
6. Applying a model option is centralized here to ensure consistent construction of provider instances with resolved temperature, max_tokens, and context size.  
7. The code contains safeguards around base_url handling to avoid accidentally reusing a default provider's base_url for other providers.  
8. Session metadata handling and optional persistence via a session manager allow selections to survive across restarts or be scoped to sessions.  
9. Exposing runtime model details supports status reporting and user feedback after changes are applied.  
10. Error handling in model activation is localized so callers receive clear messages when instantiation or switching fails.  
11. The mixin keeps provider creation and global provider switching responsibilities separate by delegating to create_provider and set_provider.  
12. Overall, it exists to make model selection robust, auditable, and easy to integrate into agent command workflows.  

Key elements:
- AgentModelMixin class (mixin providing model selection behavior)  
- _normalize_provider_key(provider) (maps aliases like "chatgpt" → "openai")  
- _refresh_runtime_model_details(source, model_id) (populates self._runtime_model_details from provider or config)  
- get_runtime_model_details() (returns copy of runtime model details)  
- get_allowed_models() (builds allowlist from cfg.model.allowed with fallback to cfg.model)  
- _resolve_allowed_model(selector) (resolves selector by id, #index, provider:model or model name)  
- _apply_model_option(option, source, model_id) (creates provider instance and switches runtime provider)  
- _session_model_selection() (reads model_selection from self.session.metadata if present)  
- _apply_default_config_model_if_needed() (applies configured default model unless an override is present)  
- set_session_model(selector, persist=True) (async; applies selection, updates session metadata, persists via session_manager)  
- get_config (external call to retrieve configuration)  
- create_provider (external call to instantiate provider)  
- set_provider (external call to set global provider)  
- self.provider, self.session, self.session_manager, self._provider_override, self._runtime_model_details (notable instance attributes used)  
- datetime.now(UTC).isoformat() (used to timestamp session metadata updates)  

Key logic:
1. The mixin begins by normalizing incoming provider names through a small aliases dictionary to map common names to canonical provider keys.  
2. Runtime model details are constructed by preferring runtime provider attributes when available and falling back to values from get_config, then stored in self._runtime_model_details.  
3. get_allowed_models iterates over cfg.model.allowed entries, extracts or derives id, provider, model, base_url, temperature, and max_tokens, and skips entries missing provider or model.  
4. If the configured allowlist yields no valid options, get_allowed_models returns a single default entry derived directly from cfg.model values.  
5. _resolve_allowed_model attempts multiple matching strategies: exact id match, numeric index when using a leading '#', and pattern/substring matching for provider:model, provider/model, or just the model name.  
6. When applying an option, _apply_model_option normalizes the chosen provider and the configured provider, and compares base_url values to determine whether to use, fallback, or drop base_url to avoid leaking a default-provider base_url into a different provider selection.  
7. Temperature and max_tokens resolution uses option-specific values when present or falls back to cfg.model values, converting to float and int respectively before provider instantiation.  
8. The provider is instantiated via create_provider with resolved parameters including api_key, base_url (or fallback), temperature, max_tokens, and num_ctx from cfg.context.max_tokens, then set_provider is called to switch runtime state.  
9. After provider creation and switch, runtime model details are refreshed and stored so callers can display current provider/model status.  
10. Session selection logic reads structured model_selection from session.metadata when present, validates provider and model, and returns a normalized dict for use in applying a session override.  
11. set_session_model orchestrates user-driven selection: handling empty selectors and "default"/"config" resets, resolving selectors, applying options inside a try/except to surface activation errors, updating session.metadata with model_selection and an ISO timestamp, and optionally persisting via session_manager.save_session.  
12. Throughout, the mixin returns clear boolean and message tuples for the async set_session_model flow, enabling callers to present success or failure feedback.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/execution_queue.py

Description:
1. This module implements lane-based asynchronous execution queue primitives for in-process task management.  
2. It defines built-in lane identifiers and specialized exceptions to indicate when a lane has been cleared or when a task result is stale.  
3. QueueEntry and LaneState dataclasses capture queued task callables, futures, timing metadata, and per-lane concurrency state.  
4. CommandQueueManager provides per-lane concurrency control, enqueueing, draining, task lifecycle handling, and introspection utilities.  
5. Tasks are scheduled and executed using asyncio.create_task and their completion or failure is propagated to associated futures.  
6. The manager assigns incremental task ids, tracks active_task_ids, and prevents stale completions via a generation counter.  
7. The file also defines follow-up queue primitives (QueueSettings, FollowupRun, FollowupQueueState, FollowupQueueManager) for deferred prompt handling.  
8. Followup queues support different modes (collect, steer, interrupt, steer-backlog) and drop policies including summarization of dropped items.  
9. Utilities are provided to normalize lane names, queue modes, and drop policies from various textual inputs.  
10. Logging hooks are used throughout to warn, debug, and report errors during enqueueing and draining operations.

Purpose:
1. The file centralizes robust asynchronous queuing behavior so different application components can schedule work independently and with predictable limits.  
2. It exists to provide a lightweight in-process mechanism for isolating contexts via lanes, each with its own queue and configurable concurrency.  
3. By modeling queued tasks and lane state explicitly, the module enables precise tracking of waiting and active work and supports warning callbacks for long waits.  
4. The generation mechanism and specialized exceptions exist to safely invalidate or ignore task completions after explicit lane resets or clears.  
5. CommandQueueManager offers convenience methods for common operations: enqueueing into a lane or the default main lane, adjusting concurrency, and clearing work.  
6. The follow-up queue manager complements the command queue by buffering and coalescing follow-up prompts when a session is busy, reducing noise.  
7. Drop policies and dedupe modes let callers balance responsiveness against resource limits, choosing whether to drop new items, old items, or summarize lost items.  
8. Debounce behavior and collect-mode composition are included to build aggregated prompts or summary prompts that reflect multiple queued follow-ups.  
9. The design leverages asyncio futures and create_task so callers can await results naturally while the manager handles scheduling and backpressure.  
10. Logging integration provides observability for wait warnings, task failures, and drain errors so higher-level code can monitor queue health.  
11. The utilities for normalizing textual mode and policy inputs make configuration more forgiving and consistent across different callers.  
12. Overall, the module exists to reduce duplication and complexity in higher-level code that needs reliable queuing, draining, and backpressure semantics.

Key elements:
- CommandLane (class constants) (predefined lane names: MAIN, CRON, SUBAGENT, NESTED, AGENT_RUNTIME).  
- CommandLaneClearedError (exception) (raised when queued tasks are rejected after a lane clear).  
- CommandLaneStaleTaskError (exception) (raised when a task finishes after lane generation changed).  
- QueueEntry (dataclass) (holds task callable, future, enqueue time, warn threshold, on_wait callback).  
- LaneState (dataclass) (per-lane queue, active_task_ids set, max_concurrent, draining flag, generation counter).  
- CommandQueueManager (class) (core manager with enqueue_in_lane, enqueue, _drain_lane, _run_entry, clear_lane, reset_all_lanes, etc.).  
- QueueSettings (dataclass) (mode, debounce_ms, cap, drop_policy configuration).  
- FollowupRun (dataclass) (prompt payload, enqueued timestamp, message id, summary line, metadata).  
- FollowupQueueState (dataclass) (per-key follow-up deque, debounce and cap settings, dropped/summarized tracking).  
- FollowupQueueManager (class) (enqueue_followup, schedule_drain, dedupe/drop policy logic, summary/collect builders).  
- resolve_session_lane / resolve_global_lane (functions) (normalize provided lane or session keys).  
- normalize_queue_mode / normalize_queue_drop_policy (functions) (map textual inputs to canonical modes/policies).  
- External dependencies and calls: asyncio (create_task, get_running_loop, sleep), captain_claw.logging.log (logging).

Key logic:
1. Enqueue operations create a QueueEntry with a future and timing metadata, append it to the appropriate lane deque, and call _schedule_drain to ensure processing begins.  
2. _schedule_drain marks a lane as draining and spawns an asyncio task to run _drain_lane unless the lane is already draining.  
3. _drain_lane pops entries while there is capacity (len(active_task_ids) < max_concurrent), warns on long waits, assigns a unique task_id, records the current generation, and starts _run_entry as a task.  
4. _run_entry awaits the queued callable, then uses _complete_task to remove the task id only if the task’s captured generation matches the lane’s current generation.  
5. If a generation mismatch is detected, completion is treated as stale: the code logs the event and sets a CommandLaneStaleTaskError on the entry future if it is not done.  
6. On normal completion, the result is set on the entry future and another drain is scheduled to continue processing queued items.  
7. On exceptions from the user task, the error is logged and the exception propagated onto the entry future unless the task is stale, in which case a stale-task exception is used.  
8. Clearing a lane removes all queued entries and sets CommandLaneClearedError on their futures so waiting callers are immediately notified of rejection.  
9. reset_all_lanes increments each lane’s generation, clears active_task_ids, and triggers drains for lanes that still have queued items; this prevents resumed completion of tasks started under prior generations.  
10. FollowupQueueManager enforces deduplication by message id or prompt text, applies drop policies (old/new/summarize), and updates summary_lines and dropped_count when items are evicted.  
11. When draining follow-up queues, debounce logic waits for a quiet period based on last_enqueued_at_ms before proceeding, optionally awaiting an external readiness callback.  
12. Follow-up drain behavior depends on mode: collect mode aggregates multiple queued items into a single composed prompt, summarize mode emits a summary prompt for dropped items, and interrupt/steer modes prioritize the most recent item while clearing backlog.

---

## captain-claw-src/captain_claw/web_server.py

Description:
1. This module implements the Web UI server for Captain Claw.  
2. It defines a WebServer class that manages an Agent, WebSocket clients, background tasks, and various auxiliary services.
